You're hungry. Do you want comfort food, celebration, quick fixes, or family traditions? [[Comfort Food]] [[Celebration]] [[Quick Fix]] [[Family Tradition]] [[Lobster Style]] [[Background On This Cookbook]] Food has always been more than nourishment. It is memory, tradition, and connection; a language spoken across generations and cultures. This cookbook brings together not just recipes, but the personal stories and reflections of our classmates, creating a shared collection that is both practical and deeply meaningful. Each recipe included here comes from someone’s family table, holiday gathering, or late-night craving, reminding us that food is as much about people as it is about ingredients. The intent of this cookbook is twofold: first, to preserve the unique recipes and traditions that shape who we are; second, to highlight the power of storytelling through food. By weaving personal essays with recipes, we see how something as simple as a loaf of bread or a pot of stew can carry with it lessons of resilience, love, heritage, and identity. These dishes anchor us to our pasts while also becoming part of our shared memory as a class. Creativity was central to the making of this project. Instead of producing a standard recipe book, we designed ours in an online, interactive format that mirrors the way food and stories travel in today’s world, not only by hand-written notes or word of mouth, but through links, images, and shared digital spaces. This format allows us to pin memories to locations, layer recipes with visuals, and give readers a sense of being part of each cadet’s kitchen and community. Ultimately, this cookbook is meant to be a reflection of who we are as a class: diverse in backgrounds, but united in purpose. Just as every recipe requires different ingredients to make something whole, our class is composed of many unique voices and traditions that together create a complete story. We hope this collection inspires others to cook, to share, and to remember the deeper meaning behind a good meal. alt="Beef Stew" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 1 ½lbs beef cubes – brown - Potatoes - Carrots - Celery - Basil, oregano, thyme bay leaf, salt + pepper - 4c tomato juice + 1-2c water - Leftover juice from beef roast + one can of beef broth - Worchershire sauce - Some ketchup Directions: 1) Put all in crockpot and stir. Cook on high for 6+ hours 2) *on stove: cook meat, carrots, celery for 1 hour then add potatoes. [[I want to hear her story]] Back to [[Comfort Food]] The above recipe is my own digital transcription of my grandmother’s beef stew recipe. I have chosen to keep it exactly the same as she originally wrote it (the only difference is that this version is in my own handwriting). The original recipe can be found hanging in her spice cabinet on a yellow slip of paper. She is constantly adding to and taking away from it, perfecting the taste and texture of each component of the dish. She has been making this meal for decades, much longer than my siblings and I have even been alive. Over these many years, she has completely memorized this recipe, save for a few specific cooking instructions and the measurements of some ingredients. At first glance, many would think this recipe is incomplete. It is. My grandmother has been making this recipe for so long that instead of writing out every single detail of the recipe, she only includes the most recent updates to ingredients and cooking instructions. This is just one of many peculiarities that can be found in my grandmother’s recipes, carrying with it years and years of dinnertime memories and cooking experiences. These quirks serve as proof that food is timeless and holds memories. One of my favorite parts about this recipe is where she has crossed out an ingredient that she thought did not taste right and replaced it with something else. That is something that you will never find in a single cookbook. My grandmother made the choice to edit the original paper instead of starting over and rewriting it to make it look more appealing to the eye. This recipe is authentic. It is stained with food, crinkled from use, and scratched over with navy blue and bright red ink. The page is riddled with stars and lines, denoting importance or redaction. She has added substitutions and vague cooking instructions, just as a reminder for the next time she makes the dish. This is a very personal recipe, most likely one that only a member of my family who has seen her make the dish, or made the dish with her, would understand. It is that intimate aspect that sets this recipe apart from anything you could find in a cookbook or online recipe platform. This beef stew was a big part of my childhood. There was a time when my sister, mom, and I lived with my grandparents, and this was always one of my favorite things that my grandmother made for dinner. Although the circumstances under which we were living with them were not ideal, my grandmother never failed to make us feel loved and cared for, in part through the meals she made for us. My grandparents helped us greatly through this season in life, taking my sister and I to school, picking us up after practices, and providing a loving home for us all to come back to and share a meal in at the end of the day. I feel that this recipe is the embodiment of that period in my life. It tastes like love in times of sadness and smells like hope in the midst of uncertainty. I will be forever grateful for the memories and care that I associate with this dish. The personal story behind this recipe very closely parallels the very beginning of the introduction in Toni Tipton-Martin’s Jubilee. She brings the reader to a specific moment of how she “knelt on the cool hardwood floor in [her] home office,” looking through a collection of cookbooks (Tipton-Martin 11). This instant that she describes mirrors the time in my life that this dish brings me back to. When this author thinks of food, she thinks of this moment; when I think of food, I think of that season in life. Tipton-Martin describes the recipes she flips through as “secondhand[,] musty” and “antique,” similar to the way I would describe my grandmother’s recipe (Tipton-Martin 11). Although my grandmother’s recipe is old and slightly ragged, the dish that it details the creation of is timeless. Tipton-Martin speaks of the “legacies” observed through her cookbooks (Tipton-Martin 11). I believe that my grandmother’s recipe is a legacy in and of itself. It speaks of the seasons of life through which it has been made and all of the memories that accompany them. My grandmother’s beef stew recipe proves that food is timeless and holds memories. alt="Cake" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: -6ish tbsp of butter -2 cups of flour -0.5 cup of sugar -1.5 tsp of baking powder -1.5 tsp of baking soda -3/4 cup milk -2-3 large eggs -1 tsp of vanilla. Quick Instructions:     1.    Preheat oven to 350°F (175°C). Grease and flour pan(s).     2.    Cream butter and sugar until light and fluffy (they don’t have to be that fluffy). Add eggs one at a time and vanilla.     3.    Mix flour, baking powder, and baking soda. Add to butter mixture alternately with sour cream.     4.    Pour the batter into the pan     5.    Bake 35–45 min or until toothpick comes out clean.     6.    Cool 10–15 min in pan, then transfer to a plate and cover with a towel for a little. [[What's the background on this recipe?]] Back to [[Celebration]] For this recipe I chose my mom’s homemade cake, a simple, unimaginative and frankly bland recipe. Yet it is all those qualities that make it so endearing in my memories and those of my siblings. It isn’t just the recipe itself because any simple recipe could be used to make a cake that would probably taste better but it shows “how a recipe can be viewed as a story, a cultural narrative that can be shared and has been constructed by members of a community”, from the excerpt of Care Fully by Kelsey Virginia Dufresne. Getting into the details about when and where this cake came from, I don’t remember a specific date or time, it seems something that has just been in the family growing up and that we all likened to a sort of shared delicacy. The recipe itself is straightforward and if you were to make it you wouldn’t need to follow the exact measurements, there is room in the recipe to take creative liberties to do whatever you may want with it. In the same way that “cookbooks bear witness to the time in which they were created”, from No Mere Culinary Curiosities, this recipe bears witness to the time in my life it occupied. We would have this almost every month or every other month growing up and it was something we could always count on to be in the fridge, if we didn’t eat it all already. The most important part of the recipe which isn’t mentioned in the recipe itself is the cake pan which you have to use if you want to try to connect to the authenticity that this recipe was built on. I couldn’t tell you many things confidently growing up but I could absolutely tell you how many ridges this fluted cake pan left on the cake because of how often I would sneak into the refrigerator and slice off a piece with the goal of making the cake seem untouched. The smallest slices, cut at an angle to give the appearance of being the same for the most part. I was the greatest thief or maybe my mother just never said anything. This cake taught me to lie with a straight face, I quickly learned how to say I didn’t have anything to do with the missing slice. I remember late nights after long days at school, clubs and whatever younger me and my brother were doing, we’d come home and for an afternoon snack have a piece of cake and yogurt. It wasn’t the best thing ever but just thinking about it now makes me smile as if I was eating that same “meal” right now. The memories with that cake are just as warm as the cake when it came out of the oven and we’d cover the pan with a kitchen towel. I can’t even tell you why we did that, but it just seemed like the right thing to do since my mom would do it every time. When we’d have guests over and she’d offer cake and tea you could always tell that these guests had no idea how special that glorified bread sitting on their plate was to us. That original cake pan eventually had the paint start falling off and the cakes that came out looked disheveled and homeless. It was during this time that my mother decided to use a new cake pan and even started experimenting with the recipe, adding sour cream, carrots, almonds, but none of them were as important as the original even if they did taste better. It’s only looking back now that the sweetness of the memory is mistaken for the flavor of the cake. The taste wasn’t anything exceptional, but it was more of the fact that it was soft and made with effort that we got to take part in. We got to see her mix the ingredients, we got to taste the leftover batter that didn’t make it into the pan, we got to test it with a toothpick to see if it was ready. The recipe itself is secondary to the memories that are stored within its lines, or maybe they’re parts that we added on. alt="Pasta" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - Pasta (personal preference)  - Olive Oil - Ground Beef - 1 White Onion - 1 Jar of Pasta Sauce - Seasonings (Garlic, Italian Seasoning) - Parmesan - Spinach to add to the sauce Directions:  1) Sauce - Finely chop onions and saute in hot olive oil. When they get soft and caramelized, add ground beef. While browning, season with salt, pepper, garlic, and Italian seasoning. Once done, add the sauce to the meat and let it simmer on a low heat. Add fresh spinach to the dish, tear it into pieces, and let it simmer in the sauce. Taste and add more seasonings for personal preference. Top with Parmesan cheese.  2) Pasta - Generously salt the water. When the water boils, add the noodles. Cook until preferred doneness. Drain and drizzle with olive oil to prevent sticking.  3) When both are complete, add them together and top with more Parmesan cheese. [[Background?]] Back to [[Quick Fix]] While simple, this recipe was the first meal I cooked for my partner this past summer. I kept it simple for a reason. It was the end of the first summer period, and we were given a four-day weekend for the Juneteenth holiday. We had planned a trip to Estes Park, and I had never been but wanted so badly to see the Stanley Hotel on that Saturday. Friday night, we arrived at the Airbnb we rented for the night, dropped our things, and headed to the grocery store to get all the ingredients for dinner. My step-mom (a wonderful cook) sent me an easy, quick recipe to use for dinner that night: pasta. What should’ve been a quick 20 minutes in the grocery store quickly spiraled into an external dialogue of deciding which onion is the “right” onion for pasta, which pasta looked the coolest, and what specific kind of sauce we should pick. I mention all of this to say that instructions and ingredients are important. Kinds, types, and flavors are all extremely important and necessary when curating a recipe for others. Additionally, quantities of the ingredients would also have been beneficial to add to this recipe. We ended up with a lot more food than we needed, and because of our cadet dorm lifestyle, saving leftovers and extra ingredients isn’t conducive to the environment we exist in. Aside from this oversight, the remainder of the recipe was simple enough to follow; however, I do think I cut the onion entirely incorrectly. The pasta came out great, even through the quirks and complications throughout. Fortunately, there were leftovers and we were able to take these with us on the camping trip the following night. We were hiking Grays and Torreys, two 14ers, Northeast of Keystone, CO. This would be both of our first 14ers, since then we have done three more. We made it up to the trailhead and found a place to set up camp. We set up two camping chairs and used the cooler containing the food as our dinner table. We pulled out the leftover pasta and divided it as equally as we could. Grabbed a pack of playing cards from the car and played cards while we ate our leftovers. Poor planning left us with one fork to share, so we passed that back and forth while we stared up at the peak we were summiting early the next morning. This was an extremely formative experience for me, one that really pressed the boundary between food and memories. I have always found myself eating because I need to survive. I never focused on ingredients, flavors, or internal culinary curiosities. After this moment, I felt a lot more connected to the meanings behind food and what it can do for relationships between individuals. I thought back on other experiences where food was an important factor in creating memories, and it is more relevant than I thought before this experience. In relation to our class texts, I find the Joy of Cooking most applicable. I felt like I needed to be someone I wasn’t in the kitchen at first, overcomplicate things, and choose to make cooking harder than it needed to be. The Joy of Cooking pushes back against the traditional norms of hosting, cooking, and entertainment involving food. This aligns almost perfectly with my experience of cooking. For example, the author mentions informal entertainment and how chances of success “are much greater if you key your efforts to your own belongings and service rather than struggling to meet the exacting demands of (formal entertainment)” (20). While this meal was in no way under the formal entertainment category, I felt a sort of pressure (whether self or socially inflicted) to provide a specific type of environment for the experience. Migration away from the traditional roles of hosting events and gatherings was something I never really captured. I felt as though there was a status quo that had to be met when volunteering to serve others. Fortunately, this meal shifted my thinking to be more centered around what I hope to get from the experience. My role as a hostess shifted to one that was “deliberately unobtrusive,” as mentioned in The Joy of Cooking (20). Moving forward, I will guide my actions and decisions based on my own personal preferences, as opposed to what I believe the expectation to be. This should allow for a more social, memorable, and exciting experience for myself and prospective guests. alt="Pepperoni Rolls" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 16 ounces frozen bread dough, thawed (thaw according to package directions) - 16 slices pepperoni - 4 slices mozzarella cheese, cut into 4ths - ½ teaspoon Italian seasoning - 1 large egg, well beaten - splash of water Directions: 1) Preheat the oven to 400°F. Line a large sheet tray with parchment paper, and set aside. 2) Place the thawed bread dough onto a clean work surface. Cut into 8 equal parts. 3) Flatten out the dough to about 4×4 inch diameter. I just used my hands to flatten and spread it out. 4) Place 2 slices of pepperoni in the center of each piece of dough (note: you can add more if you like.) 5) Add 2 slices of the cut cheese on top. 6) Sprinkle the cheese with the Italian seasoning (you can add as much or as little as you like). 7) Fold the two outer edges over the filling, then tightly roll, pinching the seams together until secure. 8) Place seam side down onto the prepared sheet tray. Repeat with the remaining rolls, and place them not touching on the tray. 9) In a small bowl whisk together the egg and water. Brush the exposed bread dough lightly with the egg wash. 10) Bake for 10-12 minutes until golden brown. Let cool for about 5 minutes on the tray before serving. Serve with pizza or marinara sauce, if you prefer. [[What's his story?]] Back to [[Quick Fix]] As a kid, the aroma of mom’s pepperoni rolls always made my mouth water when it drafted from the kitchen into my room. Although it’s a simple snack to make, my family would only serve pepperoni rolls on special occasions, and always for dinner. The ingredients are few and cheap, perfect for big gatherings where an abundance of food is necessary. As Irma Rombauer says, “Do not hesitate to serve simple foods for company” (Rombauer, 24). My mom would always use this recipe for parties and pre-basketball game day meals; inexpensive and easy to make, but delicious. Kim Ritchie’s pepperoni rolls were renowned across my middle and high school for being the tastiest meal. Kids would come up to me on game day and ask if my mom was making them. They would praise her cooking endlessly despite how simple the recipe was. During the pregame meal, we would all eat too many rolls and end up starting the game sluggishly. But during our middle school years we went undefeated, and my freshman season ended in one loss (I didn’t play past freshman year). The coaches all attributed our wins to my mom’s cooking but in hindsight I think it did more to worsen us than it did help us. There were parties too. Sunday night football parties, high school parties, mom’s famous neighborhood “roofie” nights (not actual roofies; just a lot of limoncello shots). Some people came for the drinks, but most came for the food and friends. Growing up in Kansas, Midwest culture was very much an aspect of life. Our parties consisted of neighbors that walked through our backyard to get to our house and let themselves in once there. They were always polite, asking how they could help prepare or how they could help clean once they left. They loved our family, and they loved my mom’s cooking. At every party, Kim Ritchie’s pepperoni rolls were a massive hit. Mom’s best attribute is that she cares so much about other people. However, she always overcooks for that reason. She made pepperoni rolls thinking that each person at the party would eat eight rolls. She would overprepare for every party. In doing so, she follows what Irma Rombauer says in joy of cooking, “Satisfy yourself that you have anticipated every possible emergency—the howling child, the last-minute search for cuff links, your husband’s exuberance, your helper’s ineptness, your own qualms. Then relax and enjoy your guests” (Rombauer, 15). She would cook for more guests than she invited and make sure to have options that suited everyone’s taste. Her personality would flip so fast after the food was done as well. She would go from yelling at me, my brother, and my dad to “give her space” to entertaining our guests in the sweetest way. No matter what though, she had my family and our guests in mind. After the party, there were always leftovers and anything that wasn’t given away to our neighbors was mine to eat. The recipe lasts for up to three months in the freezer if you store them right but for my family, they normally lasted a week before they got eaten. In that way, life always felt like a party for me. There were sad times too though. Deaths in the family, deaths of family friends, surgeries, and broken bones all called for the creation of pepperoni rolls. My mom always said that when you are sad you don’t want to eat out. You also don’t want gourmet food or overcomplicated food. She would always give pepperoni rolls to grieving families or to us when we felt really sad. It was the perfect meal, simple but thoughtful. Now, anytime I go home for a break from college, my mom makes sure to make me pepperoni rolls. It’s not quite the same without a big party or a gathering of friends, but they still taste amazing. So, whether you want to brighten up a sad spirit, or you want to make a great event greater, make the simple, tasty pepperoni rolls! wudrV35lR/WrRw4rhsUKDZQfjF87W4/7nP5AVA97Etuyr/2rJ+rTWp4Nd8OhRazFbh7N8bs3oSY+g0qa1gVXYAVp+FS8GhRLKXhCu5aueBXRYoUQpi3T1tVcWxUi2alAsqLZqRbdW1s1V4vifBt5h8R0WdpgnX00oSairYGx1x1QZnIUdyYFcoJvYt2YszFie/btGwilWJ+W70ivM9tvUwjyn2P5UqVdTL+w/wBGURnXllT7Vj4pjB9NvpSpV88xfsIfIG72twz6Vr4JRFdpV7Twv8qP6IWVseomtLCWwBtSpVuQGWgtMiu0qsQ4VFcy0qVVYUNyillpUqDCdCiu5RSpUAiyiu5RSpUCDgopwFKlUIdAoc5wYxbHTzH5iP1NKlWbyv8ALZV9Am9KlSrnIUf/2Q==" alt="Dirty Rice" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 1 to 1.5 pounds of ground beef - 2-3 bags of microwave instant white rice - 2 cans of French style green beans. - white table salt - black pepper - garlic powder - onion powder - Optional added seasonings: crystal hot sauce, pepper flakes, cayenne pepper, OR yellow pepper juice for additional spicy flavor. Directions: 1) Bring a regular frying pan to the stove and turn the heat to medium heat. After you unpackage the ground beef fully, put the ground beef in the frying pan and break up into pieces so the bottom of the pan is fully covered.  2) To speed up the cooking, also bring a saucepan to the stove on medium heat as well, and add your canned green beans. Lightly salt and pepper the green beans as needed, and cook until they come to a low simmer. Be sure to occasionally stir, and make sure to not let them boil.  3) As the meat begins to start cooking (color will go from pink to brown) you can begin seasoning the meat. If you use any pepper juice, be sure to add this in the early stages of the meat cooking as the excess grease from the meat will be drained later.  4) Seasoning the meat is fully seasoning to taste, so once you put your base amount of seasoning in, and the meat begins to fully cook through, taste test the meat (ensuring whatever piece you eat is fully cooked). If it is not desired taste, slowly add more seasoning until you accomplish your desired taste. Once the meat is fully cooked, and seasoned you can drain the grease by holding the lid at an angle and pouring the grease into another container, or separating the meat and grease to two opposite sides of the pan, and using multiple napkins to get the excess grease.  5) Once the meat and green beans are done cooking, turn the heat off on both and move the pans to their own unused stove eye to start on the rice. 6) Depending on your time and resources, your instant rice can be made in two ways as indicated on the packaging: either placed in a bowl with water fully covering it for 8-10  minutes or on the stove for the same amount of time AFTER the water begins to boil. 7) Once the rice is complete, take the rice out of the bags and place in large serving bowl. Pour in your drained ground beef, and green beans in the same bowl and mix!  After everything is mixed, this is where you can add any additional or extra seasonings if you prefer to, and then you are ready to serve! [[Why this recipe?]] Back to [[Comfort Food]] My favorite dish, dirty rice, is made by my mom, and it consists of only three main ingredients. She thinks it’s the funniest thing when I ask her to make it because she only made it as an ‘easy’ meal for us growing up. When I was younger my parents were very good at keeping adult business or as she calls it “grown folks business” away from my brother and I. When I was five years old and my brother was 8, my parents got divorced. Just a few months after that my mom got her official diagnosis of rare form of stage 4 cancer. As a child, I had no clue my mom was struggling to be a single parent raising two kids as all of this was going on. All this is happening and there was always a meal that she made. I thought she made it because she knew it was my favorite, not knowing that it was one of the easiest and cheapest meals she could make for us to make sure we had food to eat. This was because it filled up easy, and her exact words were “she could make it stretch”. These ingredients meant a lot at the time because of how cheap they were. Canned vegetables at the time were between $0.56 and $0.82 back in 2008. The bags of rice were between $1 and $2 depending on how many bags came in your box, and ground beef always fluctuated depending on the market, but often was about $2.30- $2.50. We didn’t eat this meal everyday, or every week, but this was a meal growing up that my mom would make at least once or twice a month, and we would always have it as left overs for lunch or dinner until it was gone. This meal normally would last us up to two or three days after she made it. It was the biggest comfort food I remember having growing up as a small child. I didn’t catch on to the reasoning behind this meal of my mom making it to save money until I got older and asked when she would make it again, or why she hasn’t made it for me and she finally told me the reason we often had it so much when I was younger. When we had this conversation, I immediately had to call my brother to see if he knew any of this and he told me my mom never told him the reason but he caught on to the reason at the same age I was finding out. Now she just a makes it if I ask for it when I come home because to this very day, it is still my favorite meal and my mom enjoys making it because it is so easy, even though we are not in the same place we were in 17 years ago. My mom’s dirty rice somewhat reminds me of chapter 1 in On Earth We’re Briefly Gorgeous but in a more positive. My mom now laughs at me when I ask her to make my favorite meal because in her eyes that was a “struggle meal” but in my eyes it was me thinking my mom is the best person in the world constantly making my favorite dish. Even though the meal isn’t fully surrounded by negativity, my mom and eye had different view points on it for years, and now we can both share that meal in a positive way with both sides of the memories involved. In chapter 1 of Lit and Food Studies it also states in the text, “As works of literature interact with food in its various stages—agricultural, culinary, and alimentary—they often traverse the boundaries between the intimate and the social as well as the microscopic and the planetary, a capacity that arguably defines the literary. It is this capacity that makes literature an especially vital and vibrant area of inquiry for food studies.” This connects with my mom and I very well in a social way and also obviously when it comes to food and cooking because it made me want to even learn more from her and how she comes up with certain food ideas and cooking methods since dirty rice was such a big and important one growing up. alt="Pasta Fagioli With Bacon" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 1 pound bacon  - 1 Medium onion, chopped  - 2 tablespoons of chopped garlic  - 2 Large (28 oz) cans of Kidney Beans (or four 14 oz. cans)  - 1 15 oz can tomato sauce  - 2 teaspoons of parsley (8 to 10 sprigs if fresh)  - Add 3 quarts of water  - 1 teaspoon of salt  - Small Pasta Directions: 1) In a large stock pot or Dutch oven fry bacon until crisp. Remove and drain on paper towels. Let cool, chop and set aside. Brown onion and garlic in pan drippings.  2) Put ½ of the kidney beans, water and all, into the blender or food processor to mash. Add mashed beans along with the remaining beans (and their water) and the tomato sauce.  3) Let simmer for about 30 minutes.  4) Add 3 quarts of water, teaspoon of salt, 2 teaspoons of parsley (8 to 10 sprigs if fresh) and simmer another 30 minutes.  5) If freezing a portion, separate the portion for immediate use now. Add a few handfuls of small pasta (tiny shells, small elbows ect.) cook until tender.  6) Freeze the remaining soup without pasta. When reheating add a little extra water along with pasta.    [[What to know about this recipe?]] Back to [[Comfort Food]] Homemade Pasta Fagioli was my family’s kraft mac n cheese. When I was two months old, we moved to Hong Kong, and my mother a brand-new mother of three kids, all younger than five years old floundered. She had no idea how to navigate the grocery stores, read the labels in Cantonese let alone make meals that her picky American raised older two children wouldn’t turn their nose up at (I would have never done that, there’s a reason I’m the favorite child). From that uncertainty, though, my mother began to lean on her old Pasta Fagioli recipe. The ingredients she swears were easier to pick out in the grocery store, no translation necessary, and it was easily frozen and thawed whenever it was needed, and it is undeniably cozy dish. Whenever the typhoons or “black rain” came to the little island, the Fleming children were warm and safe in apartment 25 B, huddled under a blanket fort, waiting for our mom to tell us when dinner was ready. We, similar to the author of A book of Middle Eastern Food, clung on to this recipe, to this dish to help ground us in our self-inflicted exile, “This meal had become ritual. Considered in Egypt to be a poor mans dish, in Paris the little brown beans became invested with all of the glories and warmth of Cairo, our hometown, and the embodiment os all that for which we were homesick.” (A book of Middle Eastern Food). Not dissimilar to the Beans of Cairo making a home in Paris, Pasta Fagioli carried all of the warmth and familiarity of home all the way across continents (although it helped that my mom was making it an in my case, truly, home is where my mom is). The dish itself is a vessel; it carried all of the love my mom could possibly pack into one dish. To try and box my mother’s love for me (and, I guess my siblings, if I must include them) into one dish would be impossible, so I’ll try to fit into two . Plus, it would be a disservice to my family to not include something sweet to be enjoyed after Pasta Fagioli. The first instance of a time where I remember helping my mom in the kitchen is when we were baking -I think it’s easier to include small children in baking as opposed to cooking because there is less guesswork and more sugar involved. Particularly on snow days, there was usually a baking activity, which in retrospect I know was a desperate attempt by my long-suffering mother to keep us entertained when school had been cancelled. alt="Banana Bread" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 3 bananas, mashed (ideally overripe) - 1 tsp. baking soda - ½ c. canola oil - 2 c. flour  - 2 large eggs - ¼ tsp. Salt - 1 c. sugar - ¼ c. nuts - 1 tsp. vanilla - 1 tsp. baking powder  - chocolate chips (measure with your heart) Directions: 1) Preheat oven to 325 degrees F, mash bananas with a fork. Lightly beat eggs. 2) Mix bananas, vegetable oil, eggs and sugar together in a large mixing bowl. Stir until completely mixed. 3) Mix in the remaining ingredients. Stir until the batter is fully mixed. Use a big spoon not an electric mixer for the last step, don’t overmix. alt="Great-Grandma Kay's Banana Bread" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 1⁄2 cup shortening - 1 cup sugar - 2 eggs - 1 3⁄4 cups flour - 1 tsp baking powder - 3⁄4 tsp baking soda - 1⁄2 tsp salt - 1 cup mashed banana - 1⁄2 cup chopped walnuts Directions: 1) Preheat the oven to 350°F. Grease a 9×5-inch loaf pan. 2) Cream shortening and sugar together. Add eggs, beat well. 3) Sift together flour, baking powder, soda, and salt (or just mix with a spoon). 4) Add dry ingredients to the creamed mixture, alternating with mashed banana. 5) Stir in walnuts. 6) Bake for 45–50 minutes. Check for doneness and cool on a rack. [[Who is Great-Grandma Kay?]] Back to [[Family Tradition]] Sunday mornings in my house were always “cooked breakfast day”. My mom would always wake up early to make breakfasts from scratch, everything from pancakes and french toast to scrambled eggs and bagel breakfast sandwiches. In the afternoons, the house would always smell like bananas turning golden in the oven. The warm, sweet scent of vanilla and banana would float from the kitchen and stay in each room, and it would let us kids know that breakfast that week was taken care of. My mom would make my great-grandmother’s banana bread on Sundays so we could eat it for breakfast throughout the week. This recipe to me is more than banana bread, it’s comfort, caring, and the way that love is handed down through food. The recipe itself is quite simple, with bananas, sugar, flour, walnuts, and eggs, but each ingredient is a bit more than just that. We would always buy the bananas on the previous Saturday, allowing what hadn’t been eaten to be used in the bread. They would always be brown by the time we used them, and even if they weren’t we would bake the bananas to make them more creamy. The walnuts weren’t always what my mother put into the bread. My grandmother always loved putting some sort of nut into the bread, whether it was pecans or walnuts, but my mother always hated it growing up. She would always bake us a fresh loaf either plain or with chocolate chips as a replacement, and I often loved it more than the original recipe. This recipe is how I first started to love food myself, with my mom letting me mash the bananas and letting me help as I grew older. I loved the act of baking itself, the mixing and taste testing, only to wait for something even better. The patience and care my mom showed every weekend was always proven whenever she set the oven timer. Food writing reminds us that recipes are not just instructions, but as a vessel for memories too. Care Fully notes that cookbooks “are a perfectly curated catalog of food, yes, but also instructional design- often infused with personal anecdotes, memoir and reflexivity,” (Dufresne 7). My great-grandmother's recipe is handwritten in cursive pencil and smudged through years of use, which is exactly what Dufresne is writing about, as lived in text. The stains of the paper would mirror the ones on my mom’s shirt, proof that recipes are meant to be used, adapted, and carried into newer generations and daily life. Every time I use this recipe to make my own banana bread, I’m adding my own presence to a story that began long before me. The history of women’s recipe books also highlights how food and recipes preserve that family and identity I found while baking banana bread. In Many Hands Hands, the author explains that early modern English women’s receipt books were created “by many hands,” representing both collaboration and continuity across generations (Forgotten Fragments 1). My family’s banana bread recipe feels like part of the same history. My great-grandmother passed it to my grandmother, who passed it to my mom, who passed it to me, with each of us leaving a trace in the process. Each of us would change the recipe slightly and pass it down through memory and remembering the changes made from a previous person. The sugar measurements have been changed, the butter being substituted for shortening and vice versa, the walnuts being swapped for other mix-ins, and putting tinfoil over the bread halfway through baking so it doesn’t brown too much too quickly. The recipe is not static, it is living, shaped by the hands who would prepare it and feedback from those who ate it. In that way, the recipe is not only a record of food, but also a record of those who care. When I think back to Sundays in my childhood, I realized that banana bread was never just breakfast. It was my mother’s way of grounding me for the week, and my great-grandmother's way of still being present in our lives. It’s my own way of remembering that food carried memories and love forward in families and generations. Recipes like this one show that nourishment is just about eating what is given to you, but it’s about care, a welcoming household, and love. Today when I pull the blue binder full of family recipes and flip the pages to find the page to bake banana bread for myself or those I love, I get reminded that food can hold the weight of family history and memories. The handwriting can fade, the ingredients can be adapted, but the essence of the recipe, the story, the love, and the many hands that carried it will always remain. In that way, I’m not just following instructions, but I am keeping alive a story told through generations. To me it means that the scent of banana bread will always mean home. Back to [[Great-Grandma Kay's Banana Bread (Chloey Rudy)]] In fact, if you were to talk about snow days in Apex, North Carolina you couldn’t do it justice unless you talked about my mom’s banana bread. Not quite sure you’d be talking about snow days in Apex unless you were there and, if you were, you would already know about the banana bread, but I digress. The banana bread is good. Really good. I distinctly remember pulling off my snow clothes and putting on my pajamas midday to climb up on a stool and join my mother in the kitchen, I was in charge of mashing the bananas (a job I took very seriously) as my mother measured and stirred. The recipe itself is written and marked up in my mom’s handwriting, as many of her recipes are calling to what I can only assume was a bittersweet time in her life, where her identity became almost solely mother. As observed in Care Fully, “the very nature of cookbooks provided an opportunity for such authorship; namely, because women’s lives were ‘absorbed by the welfare of others,’ they did not have the time or capacity to write about themselves.” My mom didn’t have time or capacity to write, though she is the most brilliant writer I know. Her handwriting lived in the margins of cookbooks and on recipe cards, small notes to herself about how overripe bananas worked better. That was her authorship, a record of a life spent caring for us. She poured so much love into her children that you could feel it, safety, joy and belonging had saturated itself into the recipes and rhythms of family life. It was impossible to grow up a Fleming child and not know that your mother loved you. Hence, these recipes are the instructions, I believe to feeling safe and warm and loved. The pasta fagioli carried us through the uncertainty and distance brought on by being in a foreign country, The banana bread brought comfort and fun. These dishes held me in my childhood; physical manifestations of how much love was around me. And I guess that’s what I have been trying to say this whole time, these dishes wont just feed you (although they’ll do a damn good job of that too) they are proof that you are cared for (by whoever makes them, even if it’s yourself) and even in a storm, or in exile, or in the snow, you are safe you are loved and you are home. alt="Lobster Bake" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 20 live lobsters (preferably Chicks, ~1–1.25 lbs each; plan 2–3 per person) - Melted butter, for dipping - Optional: salad or side dishes - Optional: rolls or bread, for lobster rolls with leftovers - Optional: aromatics/vegetables (carrot, onion, celery) for lobster stock Directions: 1) Purchase Lobsters - Select fresh Chicks from a reliable New England source for the best quality. - Plan for at least 20 lobsters for a family-style bake. 2) Cook the Lobsters - Bring a large pot of salted water to a boil. - Add lobsters in batches, headfirst, and cover. - Cook 8–10 minutes for 1–1.25 lb lobsters, until shells turn bright red and meat is firm. 3) Serve - Transfer lobsters to a large platter. - Serve immediately with melted butter for dipping. - Keep napkins handy — lobster bakes are a messy, casual affair! 4) Eating the Lobster - Use lobster crackers and seafood forks to break into claws and tail. - Soft-shell lobsters may be opened by hand. - Save the lobster bodies (don’t discard — they’re perfect for stock). 5) Leftovers - Remove meat from uneaten lobsters. - Store in containers - For lobster rolls (enjoy the next day). - For lobster bisque (freeze for holidays). - Simmer leftover bodies with aromatics to make stock. Cool and freeze until ready to use. alt="Lobster Boil" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - Lobster Chick(s) - 3 tablespoon salt - 1 stick of butter, melted Directions: 1) In a large pot, add 3-4 gallons of water and your tablespoons of salt 2) Wait until water begins to boil before adding your Chick, head down, and one at a time. 3) Place the pot’s lid on and allow the lobsters to boil for 17 minutes 4) While your lobster is boiling, begin melting your butter [[How to eat a lobster]] Back to [[Lobster Style]] Instructions: 1) Using your lobster cracker or hand, twist off a lobster claw and wrist, drain the water in a bowl. Separate the wrist and claw and use your seafood fork to extract the meat. Dip meat in butter. 2) For the legs, use your cracker or hand to break off the lobster’s legs. Turn the legs upside down and drain into a bowl. Suck the meat out of the legs. 3) Remove the tail from the body by snapping the body in half with a cracker. Push the meat out of the tail using your finger or fork. Pull off the top layer of meat with the vein, this is the digestive tract, don’t eat it. Enjoy the rest of the tail with butter. For Reference: - Depending on the hardness of the lobster’s shell you can either break off parts with your hand or you may require a lobster cracker. - Lobster crackers separate lobster’s body parts while you use the seafood fork to pick the meat out of the shell. alt="Scrambled Egg Skillet" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 4 large eggs  - A splash of milk  - 1 large green bell pepper  - 1 jalapeno pepper  - 4 strips of bacon or two slices of bologna - Salt and pepper to taste  - Shredded cheese (optional)  Directions:  1) Grab a skillet or pan and put it on the stove at medium heat. Dice the bacon or bologna into bite-sized pieces. Once cut up, put the pieces in the skillet and let them cook on medium heat.  2) On a cutting board, cut up the green bell pepper and jalapeno pepper into bite-sized portions. Be sure to take out the seeds.  3) Put the yolk of the four cracked eggs in a small bowl. Grab a fork and whisk the eggs briskly until they reach a complete yellow consistency. Add a splash of milk to the whisked eggs to make them fluffier, and then whisk again. 4) Combine all ingredients in the pan. Scramble the eggs constantly, avoiding letting them sit in one spot, or they will burn and stick to the pan. Once eggs are fully cooked, add a handful of cheese. Scramble until cheese melts, then turn off the heat.  5) Serve on a plate [[About the Egg Skillet]] Back to [[Quick Fix]] Grease snaps against cast iron. Bacon smoke threads the hallway. It was always a treat when I was younger to wake up to that scene. This recipe is from my great-grandmother, “Granny,” and it was one of the first recipes she taught me to make on my own. My mom would work two jobs during the summer and drop me and my brothers off at Granny’s while she went to work. Most times, there would be a trip to the grocery store first because Granny only had enough for herself in the fridge and maybe ate one meal a day. We, being children, always ran to the Pop-Tarts, Lunchables, or other bright, expensive snacks designed to grab kids’ attention. Of course, we were on a budget, so we rarely got those snacks. In fact, Granny would give my mom a list of ingredients so she could cook for us on the days we would be there. It wasn’t until I got older that I understood why she requested the ingredients she did: oatmeal, spaghetti sauce, noodles, eggs, bologna, sandwich bread, milk—to name a few. But with three boys going through puberty, I’m sure we would eat anything and everything in the kitchen. As a result, most of the meals she made were meant to keep us full longer. Oatmeal for breakfast was the norm. She made a big pot of it, and while my siblings don’t care for it now as adults because we had it so much as children, I still look forward to it. Lunch or dinner would be spaghetti, and you knew it was Granny’s because there was a particular sauce-to-noodle ratio that told the story of stretching food to make enough for three kids on a budget. While these meals were ordinary, the smell of bacon sizzling in the pan and bologna searing as we woke up told us it would be a good breakfast. As the oldest, she always kept me in the kitchen whenever she used the stove so I could learn. There were definitely times I didn’t want to, but I took pride in knowing I was making her job easier by not having to worry about feeding us. Eventually, she started to move more slowly and needed to rest more. That’s when she became the one who would wake up to the smell of bacon and eggs on the stove, because I felt comfortable enough to do it on my own. Granny passed away in 2020, and while I’m blessed she lived long enough to see me join the Air Force, she didn’t get the chance to see me come to the Academy and all of the success that has come with it. Despite all the new places I visit and the fancy dinners I get invited to, I sometimes feel a sense of imposter syndrome. It is best said in The Taste of Country Cooking by Edna Lewis: “Over the years since I left home and lived in different cities, I have kept thinking about the people I grew up with and about our way of life” (Lewis 15). As I’ve gotten older and met new people, I’ve learned food can be a status symbol. I have friends who refuse to eat certain foods because they feel they have “leveled up” in life and are of a higher status. Meanwhile, I sometimes long for the bologna sandwich, a bowl of oatmeal, or this scrambled-egg skillet. Like Lewis, I always think of the food I ate as a kid and gain a real sense of pride from it. Back to [[Scrambled Egg Skillet (Jevon Butler)]] Even though I grew up in Northern Virginia my family and I would go to the southern tip of Maine every Summer to visit my grandparents. My cousins from California would also make the trek over to Maine and we grew up having dark tan lines, pride in New England sports, and memories of lobster and ice cream. Over our two-week vacations in Maine, we always looked forward to the first Saturday of July. Stonewall Kitchen would throw a pancake brunch for the town and serve fresh blueberry jam. After, we would head to the beach and spend the entire day there playing in the waves and surfing. Once we returned from the beach we would have a lobster bake. I still try and go to Maine every summer and we still have lobster at least once when I visit. Lobster marks a celebratory occasion in my family, at my signing day and High School Graduation we had lobster. For both parties my grandparents flew down from Maine and came with ice chests of lobster. It’s always a special occasion when we have lobster because it’s an expensive dinner! Lobster, good lobster, is only found in New England. Even during the summer, even when in New England, prices are high. Now, if you’re in the D.C. metropolitan area it’s even harder to come by good, fresh lobster. Although it’s an expensive dinner, we don’t dress up for lobster. Lobster is a messy ordeal and melted butter stains clothes so lobster bakes aren’t fancy dinners. To give some context to the recipe, lobster also comes in different sizes and forms. Chicks are the smallest lobsters; they weigh a little over a pound. These are the ones my family buys and we normally eat two to three a dinner depending on how hungry we are and if there’s a salad or another side dish along with dinner. Chicks are about nine to fifteen dollars each, so when you buy thirty Chicks for a lobster boil, it gets pricey. There are also Quarters, Halves, Deuces, and Jumbo lobster and these lobster range from 1.5 pounds to over 3 pounds. We don’t get these but people like the larger ones as a centerpiece for a fancy dinner. Along with sizes there are also hard versus soft shell lobsters. You don’t know which you are eating until it comes time to break open your lobster. There isn’t a taste difference between them, the only difference is hard shells you need to use the lobster cracker utensil while soft shells you may be able to open just using your hands. As a rule of thumb in my family, you always buy at least 20 lobsters because you indulge in lobster bakes with your family and friends. Lobster bakes are also great because you can make Lobster rolls and lobster bisque out of the leftovers. Lobster rolls are typically eaten for lunch the next day and my family saves lobster bisque for Thanksgiving and Christmas time. To prepare for Lobster rolls and bisque we use the Chicks that we were too full to eat during the bake. Once we’re done with a lobster bake we take all the meat out of the extra lobsters and store that in a container in the fridge for later. We use the lobster bodies to create the stock for the lobster bisque. My mom always freezes the stock, and we bring it back to Virginia and store it in the freezer until the holidays come around. Lobster bisque also requires lobster meat, so we set aside a container for lobster rolls and a separate container of lobster meat that we freeze as well for the lobster bisque in the Winter. When creating my recipe, I used The Taste of Country Cooking and How to Cook and Eat in Chinese as models. I referred to the Cold Roasted Pheasant recipe in Country Cooking because it emphasized “buy[ing] your pheasant from reliable sources” and how to pick out your pheasant at the butcher (Country, 150). I discussed a similar emphasis in my recipe on where to buy your lobster to maximize quality and avoid overpriced venues. Analogous to how you don’t “wash out the cavity as it will take away some of the special flavor” of pheasant, when cooking lobster, you keep the lobster’s body to flavor the lobster bisque (Country, 150). Because The Taste of Country Cooking was a regional cookbook with niche recipes it was like a lobster recipe because of the required specificity of instructions for an audience who may be inexperienced with lobster and its variety of uses. In both How to Cook and Eat in Chinese and The Joy of Cooking books the authors explained the use of different utensils which I incorporated in my recipe regarding lobster crackers and seafood forks. Lobster is not a common meal for most people so sharing how to eat the lobster was an important part of the recipe as well. I tried to include more detailed instructions for eating the lobster, however, I feel as though nothing is better than seeing someone separate a lobster in front of you. This exemplifies how video and paper mediums of recipe instruction can work together to create the best learning environment for the audience with respect to cooking and eating new foods. alt="Mac and Cheese" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 16oz Macaroni noodles  - 1 Gallon of chicken broth - 2 cups heavy whipping cream  - 3 cups milk  - ½ c butter  - 12 oz velveeta  - 24 oz shredded cheese (any combination) - 1 tsp dijon  - ¼ tsp paprika - ½ tsp garlic powder  - ½ tsp onion powder  - ½ tsp black pepper Directions: 1) Preheat oven to 350 degrees 2) Bring chicken broth to a rolling boil, and add noodles for 5-6 minutes. Once done, strain the noodles. 3) Heat the milk and heavy whipping cream. Once hot enough, melt butter. Once butter is melted and mixed in, add in velveeta. 4) Add in Dijon, paprika, garlic powder, onion powder, salt, pepper, and stir to combined. 5) Lower heat and add half of the shredded cheese mixture. 6) Coat a 9x13 baking dish with butter. Add in a layer of noodles, followed by a layer of cheese sauce, followed by a layer of shredded cheese. Repeat this step until the pasta is gone. 7) The final layer of shredded cheese should be topped with a bit of paprika. 8) Bake for 30 minutes until the top is golden brown. [[Learn the cheesiest story]] Back to [[Comfort Food]] The dish I would like to share is my mom’s mac and cheese. This dish is typically served as a side at Thanksgiving, Christmas, or other random family functions throughout the year. The recipe begins, of course, by preheating the oven to 350 degrees. This is an important step that I rarely remember, to my mother’s annoyance. Then, around 16 cups of chicken broth are brought to a rolling boil. If you are low on chicken broth or need to save money, it can always be diluted with water. Once the broth is at a rolling boil, add the noodles and allow them to cook for around five or six minutes, depending on what the box says. Do not cook them all the way, that will happen when the dish is put in the oven. Once they are done cooking, drain the broth and strain the noodles. If everything goes as planned, the noodles should be done around the same time as the sauce. While you wait for the broth to boil, you can begin preparing the sauce. Good mac and cheese has not only cheese or sauce, but both. Depending on how much you can spend on ingredients, you can switch between a custard and a roux. For a custard-style cheese sauce, I think my mom always starts by heating the milk (three cups) and heavy whipping cream (two cups). She adds the butter (1/2 cup) once the milk and cream are hot enough to melt it. The next step is understandably very controversial; it is time to add the Velveeta (12 oz). I understand the hesitancy with such a bold step. I, myself, have tried plenty of mac and cheese recipes in which the Velveeta is, to my disappointment, the only cheese I can taste. However, when used as a texture enhancer, it is a great addition. I believe the next step is the seasoning. One teaspoon of Dijon mustard or mustard powder, depending on availability and preference, along with paprika for color, garlic powder, onion powder, salt, and pepper. Once everything is combined, my mom either lowers the heat or turns it off entirely and adds half of the shredded cheese mixture (24 oz total) she has created. It is always different. I could not tell you what to grab in the store, but if I had to guess, I would say some type of cheddar and maybe a jack of some sort. Now it is time to prepare the mac and cheese for the oven. My mom uses a 9x13 glass baking dish or a cast-iron pan, but the aluminum containers you can buy at the store will work if you are taking this dish to a function. She uses a stick of butter and coats the entire dish with it, then begins by adding a layer of noodles. This is then covered with a layer of sauce, followed by a layer of cheese. This step is repeated once or twice until the noodles are gone. The final layer of cheese should then be topped with a bit of decorative paprika. The layering is crucial to the cooking process and is essential for everything to combine perfectly. Hopefully you remembered to preheat the oven! Like I said, 350 degrees for 30 minutes. If the cheese on top has not started to form golden brown bubbles, either continue cooking until it does, or place it in the broiler for a few minutes to achieve the desired look. This recipe is one of my family’s favorites. I love it when she brings it to family events like Thanksgiving or Christmas, and when I see someone else has decided they want to give it a try, just to have the entire container they brought be packed up to go back home with them. Sometimes I feel bad and put some on a plate just to put it in the garbage upside down so they do not know people did not like it as much as my mom’s. The process of making it while my family is around the house and everyone helping in their own way, my dad’s way of helping being to taste-test everything before it goes into the oven, is always a good way to spend time. I look forward to going home to my mom’s promise of making it for Thanksgiving this year. I always miss being in the kitchen with her, or just around the house while something is being made. As quoted in Jubilee, “Food was never just sustenance, but a means of retaining history, of remembering who we are and where we came from” (Tipton-Martin, Jubilee, 2019). Thinking of this dish also makes me think of Christmas. After the presents have been opened and we are all relaxing in front of the TV with a Christmas movie on, laughing and enjoying simply being together, my mom gets up to announce that she is going to start preparing the dish to bring to a family Christmas event. I then jump up to help, as I always have, and spend time with my mom in the kitchen. Michael Pollan emphasizes the importance of this, saying recipes, “Are as much about reproducing relationships as reproducing dishes” (Pollan, Ch. 1). These memories and relationship dynamics built over the years have shaped the traditions I never thought had been created until looking back while writing this essay. I hope to share similar memories with my family one day as well. 2lv8RHJnwTtBRB4mP7Pgjch0UPFv7vIfRLcG0C7iBP6iluDaV9s86W+KAoj2JOpJ70DCMowgQenSTEw9OknQg9OmigGGhMQRqYjBa1SkWeyIaAnIigsgsRQEZUUFlTTQx2UcxJjIcyyQE5ZQBX2SVDIdRCAPHChFAWGFCYFxhQihWEbhwigCCkmJsKKaAst7NArLZITALTagQdqaEWaExBAgDDaFJTLhAjyAICBkPTYypUsRDggoogD0fJIZJCYEwgCQgQQBIEWCYFiEAXCYi6ALgIERuUmMImIvSTQi6YgrUAf/2Q==" alt="Holiday Orange Rolls" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: Rolls: - 1c milk - 3tbsp butter - 1/2c sugar - ½ tsp salt - 2 packages dry yeast - 3 eggs - almost 5c all purpose flour Filling: - 6 tbsp softened butter - 1/2c sugar - 2 tsp shredded orange peel Glaze: - 2c sifted powdered sugar - 3-4 tbsp orange juice Directions: 1) Scald milk, place in bowl, add 3 tbsp butter, add 1/2c sugar and salt. When luke warm add yeast, let stand 3 minutes, add beaten eggs + 1c flour. Beat well, add enough remaining flour make stiff dough. 2) Roll out on floured surface, knead 10 min. Place in greased bowl turning once to cover surface. Cover and let rise double (2hrs). Divide dough in half and roll each in 12x8” rectangle. 3) Mix left butter, 1/2c sugar + orange peel + spread over dough. Roll each on long side + seal seam. Slice into 1 ½” pieced. Place cut side down in greased muffin pan. 4) Let rise till double. Bake 375 about 10-15 min. 5) Combine powdered sugar + orange juice + drizzle over warm rolls." [[Why Orange Rolls?]] Back to [[Celebration]] Every family has certain foods that mean more than just what is on the plate. For my family, nothing captures the feeling of the holidays like my mom and grandma’s homemade orange rolls. They only bake them twice a year, once on Thanksgiving and once on Christmas, so the entire house seems to come alive with the smell of warm dough and citrus when the holiday season finally arrives. It filled the house in a way that decorations or music could not. The wait makes them even more special. By the time November rolled around, I was already thinking about the first bite of a warm roll. I can still picture myself as a child standing near the kitchen watching and memorizing every move they made. Those moments were not just about eating something sweet; they were about the generations of our family’s women, the togetherness, and the sense that our family traditions are still alive. My family’s orange rolls remind me that food can be more than nourishment, it can hold memories, connect people, and turn an ordinary recipe into something someone will always remember. The recipe for homemade orange rolls looks simple at first glance, but it is never just about combining ingredients. It is about the process and the care my mom puts into every step. It begins with scalded milk, to which she adds sugar, butter, and salt, stirring until everything melts into a smooth base. Once cooled, she sprinkles in the yeast and mixes in beaten eggs. She adds the flour, working it into a soft dough, and then comes the waiting: the dough must rise until it doubles in size. That is always the most suspenseful and boring part for me. When the dough is ready, she rolls it out flat, covers it generously with butter and sugar, and sprinkles the bright zest of a freshly grated orange across the top. She then rolls it up into a log and slices it into about half inch spirals. This reveals the perfect little swirls of sugar and orange. After rising a second time, the rolls bake in the oven at 375°F. Their golden tops fill the kitchen with a smell that belongs only to Thanksgiving and Christmas in our home. The final step is the glaze: powdered sugar whisked with orange juice and drizzled to melt on top of the warm rolls. When the pan comes out of the oven and the glaze soaks into every corner, it is clear that it is time to make our plates and gather around the dining room table to begin dinner. The orange rolls are never just food; they are the centerpiece of my family’s holiday rhythm. My mom never rushes them, even when the rest of the kitchen buzzes with casseroles, turkey, or pie. The rolls demand their own time, and because of that, they carry a sense of importance. As I got older, I began to help her with the smaller tasks like grating the orange rind, greasing the pans, while always sneaking little tastes of the glaze before it is poured. These small moments make me feel like I have a hand in creating something special, even though she does most of the work. On Thanksgiving morning, the smell of the rolls baking mixes with the sound of the parade on TV, and it feels like our own version of celebration. On Christmas, the rolls come after the excitement of opening presents, almost like a reward that ties the day together. They are not the kind of food you eat quickly and forget about; they slow everyone down. My family and I sit at the table, pulling apart the rolls layer by layer. To me, the rolls prove that some traditions do not need words or explanations, they live on because of how they make you feel. This recipe has always made me excited to have a family and home of my own to continue my family traditions in. The cookbook Joy of Cooking shows how recipes are more than instructions. One passage explains, “Never forget that your family is really the most important assembly you ever entertain” (15). I see this clearly in my family’s orange rolls. Even though the ingredients are simple, the recipe demands time, effort, and attention. This reflects the love she puts into our family’s holiday traditions. Another section highlights, “One of the hardest things in mass cooking is to give the food that personalized and cherished look that is achieved in intimate dinners” (24). The hard work my mom puts into her holiday cooking for our entire extended family has still always felt intentionally personalized due to our traditions. Because my mom only makes them for Thanksgiving and Christmas, they have become symbols of celebration and togetherness. The Joy of Cooking helps me understand that what might seem like an ordinary recipe is actually a way of building tradition while connecting people. The smell of sugary citrus still transports me back to those holiday mornings. The homemade orange rolls are the quiet expression of my mom’s love for her family. Joy of Cooking shows that recipes can preserve culture and create tradition. Because the rolls only appear twice a year, they stand as a reminder that the most ordinary ingredients can transform into something extraordinary. As I grow older, I know I will carry this recipe with me because of the tradition it represents. The orange rolls will always symbolize the joy of savoring the rare things in life that last far beyond the holiday table. alt="Pasta Fagioli with Italian Sausage" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - two packages of Italian link sausages - sweet onion - three large carrots - three stalks of celery - three garlic cloves(organic if you can) - 1 tbsp of olive oil - carrots - 3 cartons of swanson chicken stock - two cans of belmonte diced tomato - basil - garlic - oregano - four cans of northern white beans - garlic salt - garlic powder - garlic pepper - onion powder - two to three bay leaves - dried sweet basil - fresh graded Italian cheese(romano cheese) - ditalini pasta - baguette (optional) Directions: 1) Prepare the Sausage •Take 2 packages of sweet Italian link sausage. •Remove the casing/skin. •Fry in a pan until browned (no pink remaining). •Transfer to paper towels to drain excess grease. 2) Chop Vegetables •Dice: •1 sweet onion •3 large carrots •3 stalks of celery •3 garlic cloves (organic if possible) 3) Cook Vegetables •Heat a Dutch oven over medium heat. •Add 1 tablespoon olive oil. •Add diced carrots → cook 3 minutes. •Add onion and celery → sauté 3 more minutes. •Add garlic → sauté 1 minute (stir often to avoid burning). 4) Add Liquids & Tomatoes •Pour in 3 cartons of chicken stock (Swanson or any brand). •Stir in 3 large cans of tomato sauce (Contadina preferred). •Add 2 cans of Belmonte diced tomato with basil, garlic, and oregano. 5) Add Beans •Drain and add 4 cans of great northern white beans. •Stir well. 6) Season the Soup •Bring mixture to a hard boil, then reduce to medium boil. •Add seasonings to taste: •Italian seasoning •Garlic salt, garlic powder, garlic pepper •Onion powder •2–3 bay leaves •Dried sweet basil •Freshly grated Romano cheese 7) Simmer the Soup •Cook uncovered at medium heat for about 1 hour, stirring occasionally. 8) Cook the Pasta •While soup simmers, cook ½ box ditalini pasta until al dente. •Drain, rinse with cold water, and set aside. 9) Combine & Finish •Taste test soup and adjust seasoning. •Add browned sausage back into the pot. •Stir in cooked pasta. •Simmer together 15–20 minutes for flavors to blend. 10) Serve •Serve hot with fresh baguette. •Top with extra Romano cheese if desired. [[Ask about her recipe]] Back to [[Comfort Food]] Pasta fagioli is a recipe I know by heart, but I still call my grandma every time I need it just to hear her tell me how to make it again, plus, she always changes a little detail, and I like to stay up to date on the latest version. Passed down generations from Sicily, my sister and I would eat this for every meal until it was gone, it is that good. It starts with two packages of sweet Italian link sausage, skinning them and frying them in your best pan until brown, no pink! Transfer that to paper towels to remove excess grease. Next, dice your veggies: a sweet onion, three large carrots, three stalks of celery, three garlic cloves, organic if you can. Once diced, throw it into a Dutch oven and turn the stove to medium heat. Once warm, throw in a tablespoon of olive oil and add your carrots, cook for three minutes. Then add your onions and celery and sauté for another three minutes. Lastly, add the garlic and sauté for another minute, stirring frequently as garlic burns easily. Then you add your stock, three cartons of Swanson chicken stock but Grandma says, “whatever you can get on sale, it’s all the same shit.” Stir and add three large cans of tomato sauce and if you can find the Contadina, that is best. Stir and add two cans of Belmonte diced tomato with basil, garlic, and oregano. Now moving onto the beans, add four cans of great northern white beans, drained. Stir and bring to a hard boil and then reduce to a medium boil. Then add your dry seasoning: Italian seasoning, garlic salt, garlic powder, garlic pepper (yes, that exists), onion powder, and two to three bay leaves (“you have to use them, they make all the difference in the world”), dried sweet basil, and fresh graded Italian cheese (Romano cheese), all to taste. Stir and cook with the lid off at medium heat for about an hour. While this is cooking, bring half a box of ditalini pasta to a boil and cook until al dente. Rinse them in cold water, drain, rinse, and put them to the side. Now you taste test, if you are happy with it, throw the sausage and then the pasta in and mix. Cook for fifteen to twenty more minutes to “get the flavors to blend.” Then it is ready to serve, Grandma always serves it with a baguette. Enjoy! I cannot remember the first time I had this soup, but I know I will never forget it. Going to my grandma’s house every summer was always a treat, and she made so much delicious food. I would always help her cook and we would laugh together and wear the aprons her mom passed down to her. I always felt so close to her and the family I never got to meet through cooking with her, and I will make the same recipes for my kids and grandkids and pass it down to them. Before I learned how to make this soup, I was sure it had to be so complicated because I was convinced that it was correlated to how delicious it is. A relatively simple recipe though, patience is the hardest ingredient to incorporate. I have never once cooked a meal just for myself, everything I eat is intertwined with my family and the same rings true with this soup. In the Joy of Cooking, Irma S. Rombauer shares this sentiment as she writes, “Never forget that you family is really the most important assembly you ever entertain” (Rombauer 15). I could not agree more, food and family go hand in hand. Another piece that lends itself to this discussion can be found in the introductions to name’s Jubilee as she refers to what her ancestors left her as “culinary freedom.” I resonated with this phrase as, although in a different context, the way that I was always encouraged to try new things and have fun in the kitchen with those that I love. lpPPnThZkb5CYwZHiitZ1cyI961/AqBQPKKxbIzCxWvZOoFAqHLtlnZ6M6unHlFXPmSHLdTQ6F9PpR7ihogkgwdwaVdWI6j3H6U/pz8NTnfqK1lvxEhabAvtXa20nbfr/pF6+augHxqaLjz/AFqmQkTxKQBuT4CoXU22Hj1q0KixQ7p8jWXD7oOKYJkGCDXKGxcHpPSrDY7p/wD5/kaqlRTpi3cFenhtPnGUkBRmAACBy9K8Zwifsmwv41cxDYjbcD8qpzCbUMKt5C9hwVE+lcpcUnYny/8ANTum6fKfxqInvDyNeq57vkic1KfmBqynMUnn71RdaBSDF5FeAVhUd0LUQYWRix1FSHFpjegShaajVsDzrwU+MPUIeGKHWolY4A70FbTO9RNoAJjrXtJ8ZhcDuhxWaJ6ioV5kbwKDPoATbmb13hFGNzvFbo8YHaS27iVnn7VwErP8I8pn03qU7T5VeAkCvbDiYLbmDlNlSYJEDnEflXSMINpNSqUZI5W/Cpkf1atuDJcNhQggitM4cdlKRWXMPKNpMVo3C3yioeq2cGdr9PN4mEtcUs2mk+PCnjiL5KSookbSTM6nCMgFz//Z" alt="Pasta Primavera" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - Assorted vegetables – onion, zucchini, broccoli, snap peas, carrots, cauliflower, green beans, squash, bell pepper, asparagus, cherry tomatoes etc - 4 tbsp butter - 3 tbsp olive oil - 3-4 garlic cloves - 2 cups heavy cream - Salt & pepper - parmesan cheese - Box of fettuccine or linguine pasta. Directions: 1) In a pan, heat butter and olive oil on medium heat. Finely mince lots of garlic and sauté until fragrant. Add your chopped vegetables and sauté for 10-15 minutes to soften a bit, but not cooked through. Start first with the hardest vegetables (i.e. carrots cook for longer than bell peppers). 2) While vegetables are cooking, bring a large pot of water to a boil. Salt water heavily and cook your pasta to your liking (I like al dente). 3) Back to the veggies. Add the heavy cream and simmer until reduced. Stir it a bit so the cream on the bottom doesn’t burn. Next add in your cheese. Use block parmesan cheese so it melts and combines easier (the pre-shredded stuff has some weird coating that doesn’t let it melt very well). Grate a whole block of this (8oz roughly). 4) Slowly add this until desired creaminess is achieved. Reserve the rest as plate snow. Salt and pepper to taste (wait until now for this part because the cheese is very salty). 5) Over to your pasta. Save about a cup of the pasta water to adjust the consistency of your sauce. 6) Drain the pasta and add it to your sauce. Enjoy. [[The why behind pasta primavera]] Back to [[Comfort Food]] Growing up, my mom almost always cooked dinner for the family. My dad would take dinner duty sometimes, and when the kids were old enough, we did too. Occasionally no one felt like cooking, so we’d order a pizza or get some takeout Chinese food. I bet even Gordon Ramsey orders a pizza from time to time. Regardless of who was head chef that day, we always helped out in whatever way we could (even when that meant just staying out of moms way). The more involved a recipe is, the more opportunities there are for my family to all be in the kitchen spending time together. I think that’s why I’m such a fan of some simple yet arguably arduous recipes, like making pot stickers from scratch. Making the filling and dough is simple enough, but to put them both together takes a long time. That is why this recipe is particularly important to me, because it encapsulates everything about cooking that is important to me. Aside from it simply being delicious, it is a meal my mom has made for me countless times and is a product of her love and care. When I go home now, it’s a staple meal I always ask for, and never once has my mom been unable to cobble it together with what we have in the fridge. With it being a relatively simple recipe, everyone can help with it, which makes it just as much about the experience as it is the meal itself. In the Joy of Cooking, this concept is foot stomped saying, “Never forget that your family is really the most important assembly you’ll ever entertain” (Rombauer et al. 15) Whether that’s alone, or with their help, cooking can be entertainment for all. A much more shallow reason that I love this recipe is because of its throw-together nature. In its native country, primavera was meant to highlight seasonal vegetables, being a conglomeration of whatever was fresh at the time. Prior to grocery stores being so accessible, recipes were constrained by what was in season, or preserved (which can alter the texture and flavor). This was a fact well known to cooks of the past, such as Edna Lewis. In her book, The Taste of Country Cooking, she speaks on the fall fruit of plums, saying “They deteriorate rapidly by drying out and losing their tart flavor. Therefore, get your supply early in the season” (Lewis 166). While there is technically a recipe listed above, my mom said she never really used one. In fact, when I called her to ask for the details, she gave a vague list of ingredients and had to think/guestimate the quantities she was working with. The basis for this meal in our house has been to use whatever you have on hand. This makes it unique each time, thought still the same enough to love it just as much. Because there’s not a rigid recipe, that makes it fun to play with and hard to mess up. If we find a vegetable in the fridge or freezer, we are not afraid to put it in with everything because its not forbidden by a little list on a sheet of paper. Its unique yet familiar flavor, hands on nature, and its connection to my mom is what makes this recipe one of my all-time favorites.
