alt="Beef Chili Wraps" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - Olive oil, for frying - 200g lean minced beef - 200g minced pork - Toasted sesame oil, for frying - 2 garlic cloves, peeled and finely chopped - 5cm piece of fresh root ginger, peeled and finely chopped - 1–2 red chillies, deseeded and chopped - 1 tbsp light brown sugar - 1 tbsp fish sauce - Zest of 1 lime, juice of ¹/³ - 3 spring onions, trimmed and chopped - Sea salt and freshly ground black pepper - 2 little gem lettuces, separated into leaves, to serve - 1 tbsp soy sauce - Juice of ½ lime - 1 tsp sesame oil - ½ red chilli, thinly sliced - Small bunch of coriander leaves, chopped - 1–2 tsp fish sauce, to taste - 1 tsp light brown sugar - 1 tbsp olive oil Instructions: 1) Heat a large frying pan and add a little oil. Mix the minced beef and pork together. Season with salt and pepper and mix well to ensure the seasoning is evenly distributed. Fry the mince in the hot pan for 5–7 minutes until crisp and brown and broken down to a fine consistency. Drain the crisped mince in a sieve – this will help it stay crispy. Set aside. 2) Wipe out the pan and add a tablespoon of toasted sesame oil. Add the garlic, ginger and chilli. Fry with a pinch of salt and the sugar for 2 minutes. Add the drained mince and stir to mix. 3) Add the fish sauce and heat through. Stir in the lime zest and juice, then add the spring onions, stirring for 30 seconds. Turn off the heat. 4) Mix all the dressing ingredients together and adjust to taste. 5) To serve, spoon some of the mince mixture into the lettuce leaves, drizzle with a little dressing and serve. [[What are beef chili wraps?]] Back to [[Comfort Food]] This recipe isn’t something that was passed down from a great grandmother or a tradition that has been in the family for many generations. In fact, my brother found it in a Gordan Ramsey video at one in the morning during a youtube binge session. He was going through a phase where he was convinced he would be the next Gordan Ramsey, and he watched videos everyday on how to cook every recipe under the sun. He became a skilled chef with the amount of exposure he gave himself, and I love anything and everything he cooks. To this day, the best steak I have ever had was from my brother messing around in the kitchen at midnight. Back to the lettuce wraps, my brother made them the next morning and I decided it was the best thing I’ve ever tasted. It quickly became a family favorite. Almost every time I go home I make this recipe, and it has morphed into a comforting ritual that reminds me of home. Through making it over 20 times to date, I know the exact amounts of each ingredient needed, the timing between cooking each part, and what to add to make it more or less spicy. I love the ritual of chopping, even though I could probably buy everything precut. Something about doing it myself feels more authentic and homemade. Though I enjoy the comfort of cooking on my own, cooking this dish with other people is another type of wonderful experience. In sharing this recipe with friends and family, I’ve gotten new ideas on how to elevate the flavors of the lettuce wraps, or to try something new. My friends suggested adding paprika to the meat, and my aunt offered a different type of sugar to add to balance out the bitterness of the lime. Through these ideas, I think that sharing this recipe is the true joy of cooking. My family isn’t the type to sit down and have family dinners. Yet whenever I cook the lettuce wraps, we always sit down together and enjoy each other's company over food. Like I mentioned before, I love the process of cooking this recipe, but I get the most fulfillment out of spending time with my family and showing my love through cooking for them. Backing up to look at the lettuce wraps through a different lens, I have the advantage of being able to make the lettuce wraps anytime and anywhere I want. I live in a generation and in a setting where food from all over the world can be shared. In Edna Lewis’s “The Taste of Country Cooking”, she discusses how she does not have the privilege of quick access to food. She describes in detail how to prepare a hunted pheasant, something I have never had to do, and probably will never have to do. She tells us how to “let the pheasant hang in a cold place in the feather” and “remove the lungs and entrails from the inside chest cavity” (Lewis 150). Lewis and the people in her community were well acquainted with the food they had because they interacted with it at every level, from when it was a living animal, to when it was on their plate. They handled the mental labour of thinking about how they would hunt for the meat, who would prepare it and keep it fresh, and how to grow the vegetables that would end up on their plate over the course of an entire year. On the other hand, I do not have to interact with food nearly as much as her community. I do not have to catch and gut a pheasant, I can just take a quick 10 minute trip to the grocery store and have the meal ready within the hour. I can buy any meats or vegetables I want without having to think much about where they came from. Food can be such a different experience for people across time and across class. I recognize the privilege I have to be able to cook the lettuce wraps, because Lewis and her family probably never had the resources to cook a recipe like this. The fact that I can cook my lettuce wraps anytime and any day I want speaks to the vast differences in the accessibility of food to different people. Even though people have vast differences in how they’ve experienced food, I do believe that food has the power to surpass those differences. To me, this food is connection, and in more than one way. It is a connection to my family through providing a vessel to enjoy each other’s presence, but also a connection to Edna Lewis’s experience. In “The Taste of Country Cooking”, she emphasizes the connection that food brought into her family. “Each family had its own special talents, its special humor, but they were bound together in an important way… I realized how much the bond that held us had to do with food” (Lewis xv). Lewis makes it clear how much of an impact food had on her childhood, and her relationships with the families around her. They cooked and ate together, food being a central point of celebration and bonding for her town. Lewis and I grew up very different, myself in a predominantly white suburban area where I buy everything prepackaged and precut, while Lewis grew up in a black community on a farm, hunting and growing everything they ate. We have such different experiences and such different outlooks on life, yet we can share the experience of food being a connecting point for our families. Lewis’s family sat around a table, laughed, shared stories, and ate together just as my family does in modern day. Food is powerful in giving us both this shared experience, giving humans the ability to relate to each other in a way that surpasses barriers such as race, class, and time. pxAZkhmJpaMjIT0AZl0w/g0V/OL1/KObxkoUB3Iv5mMjIaAIwtSQdkpI8SVftHWkNGRkAEKaaW3UoeTD9zHKpQoWrf4GMjIAJMOsmhNC/wB0iTCLLp6Et6D1tGRkIDmasggC39jGRkZCA//Z" alt="Mimi's Biscuits and Gravy" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 1 Pork Sausage Roll (Jimmy Dean) - Flour - 1 Can Evaporated Milk - 1 Cup Whole Milk - Salt - Pepper - Butter - Biscuits (“Butter Tastin’ Grands! Flaky Layers”) Directions: 1) Preheat oven and cook biscuits in oven according to described on biscuit container 2) In a pan, brown the sausage until fully cooked 3) Cover the sausage in flour 4) Once covered in flour, quickly add the entirety of the can of evaporated milk and cup of whole milk 5) Cook the mixture on low heat until desired thickness is acquired 6) Add salt and pepper to taste 7) Add butter to taste (Usually 3⁄4 of a stick) 8) Allow guests to add the amount of gravy onto their biscuits [[Learn about Mimi]] Back to [[Family Tradition]] I once thought that this meal was something my grandmother invented herself, a figment of her imagination. That is how much I believed in her. She cooked with such ease, never needing a cookbook sprawled out on a counter, but always wearing her classic apron. We called her Mimi. She was a Southern belle from the deep South of Virginia, a wonderful cook, and, more importantly, the most loving grandmother. Eating Mimi’s food was the first time I was able to feel love through food. That feeling was so strong that my siblings and I didn't just want to eat her meals; we wanted to learn how to make them. She never minded the mess we made in her kitchen. We’d carelessly toss flour onto the floor while helping with her famous chicken puffs, or accidentally dump a whole box of baking soda into the cookie batter. Mimi didn't mind, because to her, food had character. Though we didn't visit often, trips to Mimi and Papa’s house in the Northern Neck of the Potomac River always felt special. Their home sat right off the water, where the soft waves seemed to mirror the peace we felt with our grandparents. In “Care Fully,” by Kelsey Virginia Dufresne, she explains that “cookbooks become participatory texts, inviting collaboration and making, rather than relying on comprehension alone” (Dufresne 18). Mimi embraced this sense of collaboration with my siblings and me. As soon as we arrived, we knew Mimi would be in the kitchen, laying out ingredients and appliances, with three paper towels neatly placed in front of the kitchen stools for us. As we grew older, my sister and I realized we didn't want to wait for the special visits to make Mimi’s meals. So, the time we spent at their house was no longer spent mindlessly throwing ingredients together, but memorizing them, hoping to carry them with us into our own home and share them with our friends. On January 7th, 2023, Mimi became my guardian angel. The first year without her was incredibly hard. Family traditions felt emptier, and even her famous dishes seemed to lack the love she once poured into them. What we had left were her handwritten recipes, scribbled down in an empty recipe book she had kept in the kitchen, stained with ingredients she had used to make her dishes. They weren't from cookbooks or passed down through generations; they were hers alone. She rarely included measurements or times; her recipes relied on her instincts and visual readiness. That’s why it was so important for my sister and me to learn by watching her. You could only call it “Mimi’s recipe” if you made it exactly the way that she did. In fact, the recipe isn't from any cookbook that she had bought; it was all made up in her head, which she had taught my sister and me to do. According to her biscuit and gravy recipe, she uses store-bought biscuit dough instead of making the dough handmade. Before she passed, I once asked her why she didn't make her own biscuits to go with her homemade gravy. She told me, “Don’t fix what isn't broken.” For her, it wasn't about every ingredient being handmade; it was about the love she put into whatever she prepared. In “No Mere Culinary Curiosities,” by Kathryn G. Matheny, she references Lucy M. Long when she says, “unlike art or music, which require a certain amount of experience and skill, food is a domain of activity that we all participate in” (Matheny 4). She never claimed to be the best cook, but to us she was. Mimi could have microwaved a cup of noodles, and it still would have tasted like love, simply because it came from her. When it was time for her funeral, her belongings were divided among her children and grandchildren. The original cookbook that had all of her original recipes in it went to another family member, leaving the recipes remaining alive only in my sister’s and I’s memory. A few months later, my aunt gifted my sister and me laminated copies of Mimi’s recipes since we didn't receive the original version. Although it wasn't the original version, we felt so close to her being able to read her handwriting again. Now, her recipe book will be a generation cookbook as my family and I continue to pass down the recipes she created. Today, her biscuits and gravy recipe is still, without a doubt, made at every occasion in which the family is together. Holiday or not, when the family is all together, we honor our Mimi by remaking her recipes, knowing she’ll always be with us through the simplicity of food. alt="Pop Pop's Stuffing" style="width:300px;"> Ingredients: - 12 loaves of unsliced bread - 6 large white onions, chopped - 6 celery stalks, chopped - 4 (32-oz) cans chicken stock (reserve some for later cooking) - 12 eggs - 1–2 Tbsp sage powder (to taste) - 1–2 dashes Gravy Master (optional, for color) - Salt and pepper, to taste Directions: 1) Prepare the Bread (7 days before Thanksgiving) - Tear 12 loaves of unsliced bread into bite-sized pieces. - Divide evenly into 6 large paper shopping bags. - Place bags in a cool, dry space (like a closet). - Leave for ~6 days, occasionally mixing the pieces by hand to dry evenly. 2) Mix the Stuffing (Day before Thanksgiving) - Empty dried bread from the bags into a large roasting pan. - Slowly add chicken stock, working it into the bread until a moist paste forms (you will not use all the stock yet). - Stir in chopped onions, celery, and a generous sprinkling of sage. - Add a dash of Gravy Master if desired. - Season with salt and pepper, tasting as you go. - Crack eggs one at a time into a small bowl, then add individually to the mixture, stirring until everything is evenly combined. 3) Bake the Stuffing - Reserve enough of the mixture to stuff the turkey. - Place the remaining stuffing in the roasting pan. - Cover and bake at 350°F for 1 hour. - Lower oven temperature to 300°F and uncover the pan. - Cook for ~2 additional hours, stirring occasionally and adding more stock as needed to keep moist. 4) Rest and Reheat - Once fully cooked and set, let the stuffing cool. - Refrigerate overnight. - On Thanksgiving Day, reheat stuffing in the oven once the turkey is finished and resting, so everything is served hot together. [[Pop Pop's story]] Back to [[Family Tradition]] About seven to eight days prior to Thanksgiving, start by driving up to Dave’s Market and buying a dozen loafs of unsliced bread. Though I do not recall the brand of bread, Dave will make it easy for you. The entrance to the store will be flanked by mountains of loaves in orange packaging. Since no one under the age of seventy-five shops at Dave’s Market anyway, the likelihood is that most customers are there for the same thing. Grab the loaves, pay the nice old lady at the register, and get home. You have work to do. Once at home, take six large paper shopping bags from the cupboard and open them up on the kitchen table. Take your time tearing each loaf into pieces. You can figure out how big to rip the pieces, just make sure they are the right size. Evenly distribute the bread among the bags, and make sure the pieces are not too packed down in the bags. Then, take the bags and place them in the bedroom closet, next to the winter coats. Leave the bags there for six days, periodically going in and mixing up the pieces with your hand. The day before Thanksgiving, take the bags back to the kitchen and prepare for the rest of the cooking process. I don’t have the actual recipe handy, and I have not made this recipe in twelve years, so I will estimate the ingredients and their respective amounts. To continue, you will need six white onions, about six celery stalks, four 32-ounce cans of chicken stock, twelve eggs, salt, pepper, sage powder, and gravy master. If this isn’t sounding tasty to you yet, or doesn’t even sound like stuffing, then don’t make it. However, if you do want to make it, the next sequence of steps is crucial. Get the roasting pan from the pantry and wash it out if you are feeling ambitious. Chop your onions and celery and set them aside. Empty one bag of stale bread into the roasting pan and slowly add some of the chicken stock. Work the mixture together until combined, adding more bread and stock until all of the bread is incorporated. You will not have used all of the stock at this point. The rest will be needed during the cooking process. At this point, the mixture will be more of a paste. That is okay. Add the onions and celery to the pan, as well as a liberal sprinkling of sage powder. Add a dash of gravy master and mix. This step is not crucial to achieving the right flavor, but it does result in a richer color. Continuously taste while adding salt and pepper until the mixture is seasoned to your liking. Then Crack your eggs one at a time into a small bowl and add them to the pan individually to avoid adding any bad eggs. Mix the eggs in until everything is pretty well homogenous. Save enough of the mixture so that it can be used to stuff the turkey. The rest is baked in the roasting pan. Set the oven to 350 degrees, and once at temperature, place the pan in the oven, covered, for one hour. After an hour, lower the temperature to 300 degrees and uncover. From this moment on, continuous monitoring is required. Add additional stock as necessary and stir thoroughly. This process should last for about two hours. Once set, let the stuffing cool, then refrigerate until the next day. Reheat the stuffing in the oven once your turkey is finished and cooling to assure the dish is hot and ready for Thanksgiving dinner. Enjoy. Or don’t. This is not everyone’s favorite recipe for stuffing, but it is mine. I grew up living with my grandparents, and both were good cooks in their own right. My grandfather, however, made the more labor intensive and classic dishes, while my grandmother did most of the weekday preparation. I always had an interest in cooking, and I often shadowed my grandparents as they made the meals that my dad grew up eating. When I was thirteen, my grandfather got cancer and knew pretty well that he was dying. Though I had seen him make many of his other favorites many times, he only made stuffing once a year, which made it difficult to learn via osmosis. So, he and I agreed that he would make it one more time so that I could write down the recipe. That is what I did. As you can tell, the recipe is pretty simple in its component parts, and the majority of the difficulty is in the labor. This is exactly how my grandfather cooked. If he was making pea soup, French onion soup, American chop suey, or any of the other big pots of food that could nourish an army, the recipe probably called for few ingredients but required hours of preparation. He also typically doubled the amounts of garlic and onions required. Most of his cooking reflects a bygone era of dishes that most people do not bother with anymore. I know he appreciated being able to pass on the memories of his food to a new generation, especially since so many people in my family remember my grandfather through his cooking. To cook for my family using the same pans he used, and drawing from memory to recreate his catalogue of recipes is like being able to invite him back to the dinner table. I know that his food is not as good when I make it, and more than I’d want to recreate the stuffing to his standard, I wish he was still here. But he is not here, so this is the best I’ve got. In some ways, I think my grandfather’s cooking style is totally antithetical to the methods included in The Joy of Cooking, as he made no fuss about the presentation of his food. If soup were on the menu, he’d put a ladle in it and have everyone fill their own bowls. There was no specialized silverware for the retired ironworker. The spoon you got was the spoon that was in the drawer. The connection between my grandfather and Irma Rombauer, however, is the immense level of care expressed for those around them. On page 16, Irma says “A piece of sculpture scaled to your table makes a charming base for a centerpiece. Surround it with ivy…” This is something my grandfather would never do, but it reflects a level of effort at which he most certainly operated. Even though he did not worry about the presentation of his food, he would spend months growing tomatoes and other delicacies to include on the table. My own account of the stuffing recipe reminds me of The Taste of Country Cooking as the food is a vehicle for a memory just as much as it is a recipe. Edna Lewis describes her Race Day Breakfast along with her memory of “the women busy selling raffle tickets and home-baked foods to raise money for local charities” (Lewis 146). I remember the process of buying the ingredients and feeding our dogs scraps just as much as I recall the tastes and aromas. Perhaps the author of a recipe enjoys a privilege of connection that the reader cannot understand. No matter how much detail I provide, I cannot quite replicate the experience of my childhood for anyone. Yet, when I think back on making stuffing with my grandfather, I am overcome with feelings and memories. I wish I could bottle that experience and reproduce it, for it is beautiful to me. However, just as I will never fully understand the glory of race day, no one else will ever understand the savory scents and sentimental moments of my grandfather’s kitchen. Back to [[Pop Pop’s Stuffing from the Old Country (Aidan Corcoran)]]
